Orel 1998(:485), following Çabej 1986(II: 244-5) argues that the variant ullastër is from Italian olivastro "influenced by ulli". I would note Old Occitan ullastre. Orel 1998(:485) however thinks the form ullaSHtër is from Latin oleaster. Çabej earlier (1960) preferred a native suffixation upon ulli "olive" in -shtër (-a-?), while Bonnet 1998(: 223) instead repackages this as a calque on the Italian. Note influence from an existing ullashtër onto the loan of Italian olivastro is also possible. Vatasescu 1997(:163) has ullashtër frm olivaster, which would be inherited only in Alb. ... , ... , ... , ɡ ˈe n t e m , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , ɟ ˈi n d  $folk, people. Per Orel 1998(: 136), plural only; masculine, as gjinde, may instead be from pl. gen. gentium -- or from some Italian variety later. Orel 2000(:27) argues it is a "later Latin" loan, but from Latin. However De Vaan 2017(:1743) and Bonnet 1998(:57, 143, 380) use gjind unproblematically from gentem, and gjind is also the citation form in Newmark 1998(: 285), not gjinde. Bonnet 1998(: 116) does observe regional differences however: gjëndëje in Southern Albania, gjind skewing toward Gheg (and lacking a nasal vowel), and gjende in Greece. Bonnet 1998(: 292) notes a "gjint". Matasovic 2024: 64 as gjinde._Borrowed from Latin into Classical Proto-Albanian_

... , ... , ... , s k ˈu l k ɑ m , >* , >* , >* , >* , ʃ k ˈu ʎ k ə , >* , >* , >* , ʃ k ˈu l k ə  $"barked willow branch stuck in the middle a meadow to show that the land, being occupied, cannot be use for grazing; after mowing, the branch is removed". Vatasescu 1997: 381. May come from Gothic. Mihaescu 1993(:474) asserts a later date on the assumption that the Latin form had -ū-, but a short -ǔ- instead seems evident in Italian scolca. Although only shkulkë is in Newmark 1998(: 814, 819), also noted (forms and definitions in Vatasescu) have been shkojkë "ramură indicând ocuparea  unei pajişti; strajă, pază" and a Gheg form shkujkë; Çabej apud Vatasescu considered the Gheg form to be older and wondered about Byzantine influence on it, a proposal not favored by Mihaescu (all apud Vatasescu). ... , ... , ... , d ˈu k e m , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , d ˈu c  $spigot, tap, bung, barrel of a gun, conduit. Attributed to ductum by Meyer. Orel 1998: 78, Çabej 1986(148). ... , ... , ... , d ˈu r r ɑ t͡ʃ u m , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , d ˈu r ə s  $Durrës. This is a NOVEL PROPOSAL though foreshadowed by Bonnet 1998(:37)'sa remarks about the outcome -s- from what looks like a Latin and Greek /kʰiu-/, rather than the expected /c/ <q>. Italian likewise has Durazzo rather than expected Duraccio; Slavic Drač. The idea here is that the Latin Durrachium and perhaps the Greek Dyrrhakhium actually overlie a Proto-Albanian /tʃ/ (using this sound as it is what is used in Proto-Albanian as the reflex of PIE *kʷ before i/e by De Vaan 2018 and Orel 2000), rather than a /kiu/ or /kju/, which is plausible as this sound was lacking in both languages. Regarding the accent, once we no longer have the penultimate -I-, then the /u/ becomes antepenultimate, and the stress on it falls out easily from this proposal! ... , ... , ... , n ˈoː d u m , >* , >* , >* , >* , n ˈy ø̯ , >* , >* , >* , n ˈy e̯  $knot. Modern form is now nyjë, fem, singularized plural of nyjë, the plural of nye. She refers to Demiraj 1985 (pp133-4) "pentru dificultățile refericitoare la evoluția fonetică a lui NODUS...", and furthermore cites Bardhi's adjective "nyesh" as from a Latin NODOSVS. Bonnet 380 as ndëjej. Orel 1998(ː286) as ndjej. Çabej 1960: apud Bonnet 1998(:253-4); Bonnet unlike Çabej finds this word troublesome in the lg > j development, explaining it in either phonotactic repair or influence from Byzantine Greek (ex-)omologew but it's not quite clear why this Byz word in particular. ... , ... , ... , ɡ l ˈɑ n d e m , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , l ˈə n d ə  $acorn, gland (equated to French gland which means both by Bonnet 1998 -- "acorn" is the only meaning supported in Newmark 1998(:452)). Bonnet 1998(: 249) -- lëndë is Tosk; Cham and some Tosk have lënde; Gheg has lênn, lend, and land (land as northern Gheg ?). Meyer 1891(:243) notes the plural lëndre in Cham/Arv in Greece, and Romanian ghindă, and Aromanian and Megl. glîndã. Çabej 1960 prefers a native etymology, connected to lënde "lens, lentil", which Bonnet 1998(:250) sees as more likely from Latin lentem itself. However, this latter lënde "lens, lentil" is not in Newmark 1998(; 452), which lists lënde as "matter, timber, acorn". It appears that form in -a was inherited in Balkan Romance, and much of the rest of Romance --REW p281-2 (n.3778). Possibly native instead, cf. PIE lenteχ. Final -ë may be analogical from plural, or bc movement into the -a- feminine class. Could also be from Romanian gʎɨndə forms themselves._Borrowed from Latin into Classical Proto-Albanian_

... , ... , ... , ... , p r ˈɛ b i t ɛ r , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , p ɾ ˈi f t  $priest, pastor. Og. praebyter. Orel 2000(:27) argues it is a "later Latin" loan from presbyter. Topalli 2017 and Matzinger 2006 assert it's from a Vulgar Latin prepto: < praebitor, cf Romanian preot. This is supported by De Vaan 2017: 1744. Bonnet 1998(: 285) supports descent from a nominative praebyter; on loss of -r, he (p285) asserts survives in non-truncated stems such as the plural priftërinj, with the -n- in priftëria and (priftëreshë "wife of Pope" (semantics??!!)) being analogical in Gheg; note also Dalmatian (Vegliot) prat alongside pretro. ... , ... , ... , f l ˈɑ k u l ɑ , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , f l ˈa k ə  $flame. Plural flakëra (Gheg flakëna). Cf Romanian flacără, Italian fiaccola. Orel 1998: 98-9 as a "backformation" of a *flakull, itself from "dialectal" Romance flacula evinced in Romanian flacără, Italian fiaccola, replacing facula "little torch". Note however Campidanese Sardinian flake (Meyer 1891: 107; Bonnet 1998: 271). This seems close to the view of Burr 1975(:63-4). Çabej 1960 argued for a native Albanian formation based on the semantic richness, arguing for an elementary creation on an "expressive base". However, as Bonnet 1998(: 272), these semantic developments are all to parallel to those seen in Romance, especially in Romanian flacără and Istriot fagla. Bonnet 1998(: 272, 379) defends the original etymology of facula here as either a Late Latin or Albanian-internal metathesis, the latter from a *fakël -- though in all of these cases, the plural in -ëra (Gheg -ëna) would have had to be innovated pretty early. Admittedly this is a productive suffix (Orel 2000: 229). Proposal that the plural is actually the inherited form, from a Romance flacula (per Burr 1975, Orel 1998 etc) embedded below._Borrowed from Latin into Classical Proto-Albanian_

Meyer 1891(:471), Mihaescu 1966(:31) apud Vatasescu 1997(:360), alas Vatasescu also notes the rival etymology in hibernīvum, cf. Aromanian arniu "wintering, hibernating", citing Cabej, Papahagi and Poghirc. Bonnet 1998(:129-30, 212, 230) meanwhile suspects a secondary suffixation in -i, but does support hibernus as the root and argues that this does indicate "that the spirantisation (of /b/) is quite ancient" (Bonnet 1998: 167). ... , ... , ... , ... , ... , ... , ... , m ˌa n t ˈi l j a , >* , >* , >* , >* , m a n d ˈi l ɛ  $kerchief, per Orel 2000: 57. This one is difficult. The Latin word had many forms, mantēle, mantēlium, maentellum, mantelum, mantīlium. Partially contaminated in Latin but Gaulish forms from mant-. But how do we explain the development here, without post-nasal voicing? We have the doublet of Byzantine Greek mandilia > Arabic mandil > Turkish  mendil > Albanian mindil (and Aromanian mandilă). Maybe Orel's right and it's regular, but it seems quite weird. Could also be a mantilia? ... , ... , ... , ˌi n w ˌi ɡ ˈi l i u m , >* , >* , >* , >* , m n̩ ɟ ˈi lˠ , >* , >* , >* , m ə ɲ ɟ ˈi lˠ  $vigil, fast. Alternative commented above.  Regular as mënjill per Orel 2000: 55. As vigilia > vëngjill/mëngjill in De Vaan 2017: 1739. Vatasescu 1997(:473) lists a *Gheg* mnjille reflecting Old Albanian (including Old Gheg, in Bogdani) mëngjille, as well as a mjille "ajunul unei sărbători”, corresponding to a Tosk vëgjile and vëngjillë. Currently using mëngjill as it seems to be the center of gravity (of sources examined thus far), and of course ɲɟ > ɲ would be the regular Gheg ND > N shift. It appears that in the REW (p712), nasals in the first syllable of Latin etyma in vigil- are unattested in Romance. Alternate PROPOSED etymology in invigilia considered below. Bonnet 1998(: 192) lists mëngjille, mnjille, vëngjille considers this to reflect Latin vigilia, with an analogical nasal under the influence of evangelium > ungjill "gospel" (perhaps also angelu > ëngjëll "angel"?); on p198 he remarks on this as one of six cases where a non-palatal lateral reflects /lj/. AMYGDALA need not be the form cited -- the REW (number 436, p30) cotes attested amyndala as a headword as well as an *amandola, and -nd- or regularly reflected -nn- forms are seen in Sicilian, Tarentine, Neapolitan, Abruzzese, Logudorese, Portuguese, French (al(e)mande via metathesis > English almond); Romanian migdală is from Greek per the REW. Going with the Romance form 3 from the REW (may consider adding the #2, amyndala)._Borrowed from Latin into Classical Proto-Albanian_

... , ... , ... , ˌi m p e r ˈɑː t u m , >* , >* , >* , >* , m̩ p ə ɾ ˈɛ t , >* , >* , >* , m b ɾ ˈɛ t  $king. Variant: mpret. Gheg: mret. Arvanite: mbret. Orel 2000: 114 -- may be imparātus (he who defends). Or imperātus (he who rules) -- Orel 1998:252.  De Vaan 2017: 1743 as from imperātor, likewise Bonnet 1998(: 38, 44, 207*. Bonnet 1998(: 38) argues it can't come from the accusative imparātōrem and thus prefers the vocative-nominative; for the loss of -(ë)r, he asserts there was a truncation, as with praebyter > prift, with the -r- surviving in Tosk mbretëreshë and mbretëria (the -n- in Gheg for these, he asserts, is analogical). Line added below for that hypothesis. Old Albanian had mpëret -- Kuvendi in Arbënit (Vatasescu 1997: 426). On phrasiological equivalences between Romanian and Albanian for this word family, see Vatasescu 1997: 425-6. Matasovic 2024: 64._Borrowed from Latin into Classical Proto-Albanian_

... , ... , ... , w ˌɑ l ˈɑː n i oː , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , >* , v ə l ˈɛ̃ ɲ , v ə l ˈɛ ɲ ,  v l ˈɛ j  $verb: to be worth. Identical to vjej "to need"? Orel 1998: 512. Orel doesn't consider them equivalent, but does note the variants vëjej and vjej... maybe the two diverge because of the semantic distinction? Likewise Bonnet 387. TODO reconsider... Vatasescu 1997(: 68)-- Old Alb had vëlej for this and vëjej for "to need". PROPOSAL: changed this from valēre (equivalent to vëjej) to valāre, on the basis of Vegliot Dalmatian valar, Romansch valair (REW number 9130). Bonnet 1998(: 203) notes Buzuku's "vœglièn" for "vlen", generally a word of perplexion for him as well, he relates it to how Cham regularly has fal and fëlej, though in this case as he notes it is much harder to explain (ː 203, 298, 350, 366) -- perhaps some influence from fëlej even?
